B00979CS50|5.0|I would assume that most NY&CH fans already possess this 2 CD set. Occasional fans may be holding off, wondering if this release fits in the bin of essential Neil Young recordings.<br />Yes, it does.<br />Before Neil sings the lyrics in \\"Driftin' Back\\" that make it plain, Psychedelic Pill had me thinking of the similarities between this artist and Pablo Picasso. Both are masters of an art form of which they are the central figure, each primitive in their approach. Both have had an extraordinary impact on scores of artists who followed. And both have created many influential works that much of the population doesn't necessarily embrace. If Neil had not already used the title Ragged Glory on a previous release, it would perfectly fit this work.<br />I have found Psychedelic Pill alternately indulgent, inspired and irresistible, many times within the same verse or section of improvisation. Both the lyrics and the musicianship could occasionally be mistaken as the efforts of a beginner; for an artist in his sixth decade of recorded output to accomplish this effect is remarkable. Some of the music skates right on the edge between childish and childlike, but out of this effort springs some transcendent music. We are listening to a master, alternately channeling the sensibilities of a savant and a shaman. The repetitive aspects, the raw primalism, the intractability of vision is entrancing but may not sit well with everyone.<br />Then one hears \\"For the Love of Man\\"; a heartbreaking, beautiful song, one that exposes the very depths of love, sadness and acceptance. By itself, reason enough for any Neil Young fan to add Psychedelic Pill to one's collection.<br />Great Crazy Horse music. Monumental Neil Young.

B009439HEY|4.0|Generally, I've grown pretty tired of all the remastering, re-imagining, re-recording and rejiggering of classic albums and artists these days. But I figured two things with this second set of Hackett re-visits: 1) in a world of Nicki Minaj and American Idol crap, we'll probably never see music this good created again, and 2) if you're the artist who (co-)composed the material, you get a pass when you do a deja-vu on it. However, I think this one could have been pared down to one great album because half of the songs are pretty much note-for-note recreations with little variance, while others offer something that the original didn't: a female vocal for \\"Ripples\\", a longer acoustic guitar intro for \\"Blood on the rooftops\\", a sweet Steven Wilson vocal take on \\"Can Utility\\" for instance. Otherwise, I didn't feel a need for an exact reproduction of \\"Entangled\\", or \\"Afterglow\\" (other than John Wetton's nice vocal), and something as epic (and long) as \\"Supper's Ready\\" is really better left to the originators. Still, throughout this 2-disc set, which also includes several Hackett solo pieces, the varying band members all perform with great dexterity on these usually-difficult compositions, and the vocalists don't make you scream out \\"Where's Phil/Peter??\\" (additional singers here include Neal Morse from Spock's Beard and Mikael Akerfelt of Opeth). But the real reason I can give a high grade to this material that I already know like the back of my hand: Hackett's playing throughout is exemplary, and he sounds as good as ever, his soaring leads making the whole trip that much more worth taking.
0743270754|5.0|I've never been a big fan of this author, though I'll admit to also having never read any of her books. I have little patience with wealthy successful authors who get caught up in plagarism scandals, where they copy from someone else's work, and make more money. She also spoke up rather sharply criticizing Robert Caro, when the first volume of his LBJ bio came out. Her criticism was a political version of \\"The rich are different\\"; essentially, maybe LBJ did bad things, but he was a great president, and that excuses bad behavior. So I generally had a bad impression of her. This book showed up on the table at Costco in the summer; I bought it almost without thinking, and then a couple of weeks ago, decided I shoud read it before the movie came out, because I know I want to see that.<br /><br />So I start reading, and frankly at first I'm surprised, and then pleasantly impressed. The book is very well-written, and the author essentially goes out of her way to bring to the forefront of history people most modern Americans never heard of, or at least know much about. The first section of the book (rather less than half) covers the prewar careers of four men: Lincoln himself, his Treasury Secretary Salmon P. Chase, his Secretary of State William Henry Seward, and his Attorney General Edward Bates. The author follows them right up to the Republican Convention in 1860, and then through the Presidential election. The rest of the book covers the Lincoln Administration, discussing how the cabinet handled things, what they discussed, and at least in some depth discusses the rest of the members of that cabinet, mostly Edwin Stanton (Secretary of War), Gideon Welles (Navy Secretary) and Montgomery Blair (Postmaster General). The author has a number of wonderful anecdotes to tell, from Stanton cutting Lincoln out of a legal case in the mid 1850s and then a decade later admitting he was \\"never more decieved\\" than that day, to Welles' distress with the various disagreements in the cabinet, and the feud between Chase and Monty Blair and his family, which went on essentially for as long as either man was in the cabinet. She has dozens of anecdotes, from Lincoln's story about Ethan Allen thinking that the picture of George Washington in an English privy was appropriate to Salmon Chase insisting on swearing in the first black attorney licensed to practice there when he took over the Supreme Court. It's a wonderful chatty, informative, well-written book.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the whole thing, and I think much better of the author now. Hopefully she'll produce something else interesting.

There are six seperate, yet somewhat linked stories. You get the first half of the first five stories, then you get the whole sixth story. From there, you get the conclusions of each story starting with the fifth and going back to the first. So, the book starts and ends with the same story.<br /><br />Each of the stories are written in completely different styles. Mitchell is an amazing writer because he did this so well. The language in each section appears very true to each time period from the late 1800s to modern day and to the future. Mitchell invents his own style of language and grammar for the future stories. One is told in the near future and the sixth story takes place in a post apocalyptic world.<br /><br />Mitchell is an excellent storyteller. Although the language may be difficult to get into, once you have entered the world you won't want to finish each section. Leaving a story halfway through, usually at a climax or even midsentence makes you want to continue reading the next story even more so you can get to the second half. But then, you get engulfed by the story of the new story.<br /><br />The experience of reading this book is so unique. While reading the second half of the book, you feel like you are finishing a whole book every forty pages or so.<br /><br />Not only is each story told in its own style, language, and timframe, but each is a different genre. You will definitely have a favorite story when reading this book. It may be the geriatric comedy, the post apocalyptic epic, the young musician's tragic life, the lawyer's journey on the high seas, or the journalist's 70's mystery. My favorite was the genetic engineered fast food server's tale of self discovery.<br /><br />Mitchell does an excellent job weaving in ideas of self and philosophical questions. It is up to you to interpret you these six stories fit together.<br /><br />I give this novel a 4/5. It is an excellent read, but the concept of it is so much to wrap your head around. I suggest this to anyone who loves to read all different kinds of books.s the genetic engineered fast food server's tale of self discovery.<br /><br />Mitchell does an excellent job weaving in ideas of self and philosophical questions. 0812982622|4.0|There are very few novels set in modern day North Korea - with good reason. The political regime is so secretive and alien that research must be a nightmare. It's very hard to gauge how accurate a representation Adam Johnson's book is of this but it seems to be at least plausible and generally believable. What he does so well is to move from the opening slightly smug \\"isn't the propaganda thing a bit funny in terms of what people there believe\\" to making the reader really care and understand how shocking the effects of this can be on the individual lives of the people there. There are scenes of horrific suffering but Johnson retains a light touch wherever he can - so the Dear Leader, Kim Jon iL, is presented at times as being \\"lonely\\" in a nice nod to You Tube clips.<br /><br />Johnson's hero, Jun Do, grows up in an orphanage run by his father. However he presents a mixture of people from all levels of society - there are those who believe in the myth of the leader and the propaganda, those who know the truth and use it to their own advantage and those who know the truth and use it just to survive. The challenge for a fiction writer is of course that it relies on personal stories and in a political culture like North Korea the individuality is suppressed.<br /><br />The setting is fascinating and original and that does much to offset some of the aspects that perhaps stretch belief a little. It's hard to believe that Jun Do's lack of commitment to the cause would allow him to hold some of the positions that he finds himself in for example, but interchanging roles seems to be part of the model and presumably the argument against this goes that the leadership don't even question that everyone buys in to the story and aren't just compliant through fear.<br /><br />At first the narrative is split between Jun Do's story and the official announcements of the State but later on we also get the story of an Interrogator. While this introduces a vital element to the story themixture of the three strands doesn't always feel as smooth as it could do. However, it is a fascinating subject and a highly original book that celebrates the human spirit in impossible conditions. There's humor, a love story and fascinating insight into a mysterious country. Johnson is well worth reading - this could be the beginning of a beautiful friendship, as a not unrelated movie might have put it.

The Minds are the super sentient consciousnesses that independently control the space ships in the story.<br /><br />The Minds want to help the Gzilt humanoids complete their generations long quest to rise to a higher plane of being,(from which no report has ever returned). They also have individual personalities and motivations.<br /><br />The Hydrogen Sonata is part detective story and part sheer, wonderful space opera. Mr. Banks is a tremendously skilled and inventive writer. This is not a comic book scenario, it is more like an intricate waltz. There are a few well-drawn human (and not so human) characters, but fundamentally the Minds carry the story.<br /><br />The Hydrogen Sonata is one of Mr. Banks' \\"Culture\\" series of books. B006TVQU6C|5.0|The One is my third Antec case, and I shall start with some background about my experience buying other Antec cases for system builds (since 2004).<br /><br />Antec Super Lanboy (2004) 3.75/5<br /><br />The first was the Super Lanboy, which held up decently for a case that is necessarily flimsy and light--the clue to the reason for the use of thin aluminum is in its name--it was intended to be an easier case for a gamer to lug to LAN parties, and for that purpose it was suited. When I say that it is flimsy I do not necessarily mean that it was cheap--at no point did the actual aluminum parts of the case give me any trouble at all. The side window had chrome-looking \\"rivets\\" that were ultimately plastic, and a few broke due to their cheap quality. In addition, the rear fan used rubber screws that I unfortunately didn't at first know how to remove so I kinda broke the screws (I had to remove the fan to remove the motherboard). I recall sending an email to Antec about it but I do not recall receiving a response. As it was, I used twist ties like you might find on a loaf of bread to re-secure the fan (regular fan screws wouldn't have worked unless they were secured with a nut) and had no issues thereafter, including with vibration. The last issue I recall was that the front fan died (it happens) and I had difficulty removing it. I don't remember how that turned out. But the case was 3 years old by then and had overall held up well and I had overall been satisfied with my purchase, but decided to get a new case, and was focused again on getting an Antec product. Overall: 3.75/5<br /><br />Antec Nine Hundred (2007) 4/5<br /><br />My second case was an Antec Nine Hundred, which I loved. However I got into the bad habit of tripping over peripherals plugged into the various front and back ports and destroyed a front USB port that way (as well as a couple gamepads). Over time a short developed in the front audio out port and I will wholeheartedly assume it is due to my abuse.In addition, like all cases, over time it just becomes harder to clean, harder to get at the little corners full of dust, and to really get things clean you may have to remove fans that are not so easy to get at. A long story made short I wanted to replace the Nine Hundred because my own stupidity hurt its functionality and it was getting old and dirty because my computer is on 24/7. I can't identify all of the specific little issues that negatively reflect on the Nine Hundred (such as the fact that the top 200mm fan wasn't fun to service), but in seeing how no dust is accumulating on the front of my One so far, I have to say that the Nine Hundred just got so filthy. Moving air is always good but there are consequences for that and one is dust. To be sure, I'd rather have a dusty, dirty case that is cool than a tidy case that is hot, but I'd much prefer the tidy cool case. I will admit that I used to smoke right next to the case (and lived with cats!) and that surely contributed but even when that wasn't true and it was clean as reasonably can be expected, six months or a year later it was again immensely, ridiculously dusty. Much more so than I remember my Lanboy being. Especially when using front intake fans and multiple HDDs. That was where most of the dust seemed to end up. Just caked on the HDD, HDD cage, and HDD front intake fans. Ugh. If you've read this far just realize that the only knock I have on this case at all from my usage is dust. It's quite possible that once I got it dirty with cat hair and smoke being sucked through the intakes as that I doomed the case to always being dirty given that it just moves to much air. If you feel that way, feel free to consider me to have rated it as well as any of the cases that follow to which I will give higher ratings.<br /><br />Antec Three Hundred (2011) 4.5/5<br /><br />Between that second purchase for myself and this most recent purchase for myself I built a computer for my dad with the Antec Three Hundred andit is partly the fact that the Three Hundred was inexpensive but of good quality (and clean!), in addition to reviews from tech sites that made me go with the One. If Antec hadn't released the One I probably would have gotten the Three Hundred Two (update of the Three Hundred). The only other case manufacturer that caught my eye was BitFenix and if the cost of the One is about what you want to pay and you want to look at other well-regarded options, check out what BitFenix has to offer.<br /><br />Other (Various)<br /><br />I have done other builds over the years (for others) for which I did not use an Antec case. Usually this was to save money or because a certain form factor was required that Antec did not quite meet satisfactory. In those instances, I found nothing remarkable about the case, but neither did I encounter substantial cons. While I definitely prefer Antec cases because of the features you get for the price, the build quality, and the aesthetic design (a big selling point with me as I think most manufacturers make ugly cases, most especially when they are trying to use creative designs, with a few exceptions), I can't say that there aren't other decent choices at or below the One's price point.<br /><br />Antec One (2012) 4.5/5<br /><br />I have only had this case a few weeks and the only reason I won't give it a 5 in my written review (though I will round up for the Amazon's star review) is because I can be more precise here and this case is not perfect. Dust filters would be nice, as well as expansion slots that can be removed and replaced rather than punched out, but those are minor concerns and Antec can't deliver all the features a user may want in a budget case. The features that are here--rails for drives, a location for mounting an SSD (which I do not use as the Nine Hundred pre-dated SSDs and did not have a slot--I ended up using velcro tape to mount it to the right side of the Nine Hundred and have done so in the One) are nice features, andthe build quality is nice, assembly was simple, and bonuses such as a USB 3.0 to 2.0 adapter are nice for those who don't yet have a motherboard with USB 3.0 support. I believe that a proper instruction manual doesn't ship with the case but is present on the website--however I did not need it. I have to say that so long as Antec (as well as its competitors) build cases of this quality and this feature-rich and sell them inexpensively I will prefer them rather than cases like the Nine Hundred (or its more updated models), which are 2-3 times as much.<br /><br />Final Thoughts<br /><br />If you want a well-made, quality case for a low price and are smart enough to avoid cheaper cases with bundled power supplies, the Antec One is as good as any for your money. I highly recommend this case and bought one for someone else the month prior to my purchase (I was still trying to figure out if I wanted for myself the Three Hundred Two or the One, as well as trying to possibly get the one illusion [which is identical to the One except it has more 'bling' in the form of blue LED lights]), and they like their case and have no complaints as well. Funny story: She moved her hardware from her very old case to the One and said that something was wrong. I didn't much wonder if the case was defective and with good reason: turns out she decided to pretty much randomly connect the connectors for the front panel for the power button, reset button, and LED indicators as if they are all interchangeable. They aren't. After fixing the problem, everything was fine and she loves the case.

1409144720|5.0|It's a new day--or not.  John Rebus is back in harness, working for his old force, the Lothian and Borders Police, this time as a civilian in a Cold Case Unit.  His boss, DS Daniel Cowen, resents his posting as the only serving officer supervising a bunch of over-the-hill ex-cops, and Rebus gives him plenty of attitude, further nursing the man's aggravation.<br /><br />When Rebus is approached by a woman seeking information about her long-missing daughter, Rebus isn't sure he has anything to work with; she was eighteen, and a dozen years have passed since her disappearance and the original investigation.  There was little enough to go on at the time, and since then the original case officers have either died or retired.  But the woman points out that several other women have dropped out of sight over the ensuing years, and all in roughly the same region of rural Scotland.  She's convinced there is a serial killer at work.  His curiousity piqued, Rebus decides to look into the case; but with no real support from Cowan, and lacking even a warrant card, he's very much on his own.  So what else is new?<br /><br />Rebus is unaware that he's also in the crosshairs of another cop in Lothian and Borders.  DI Malcolm Fox heads Ethics and Standards, the current incarnation of Internal Affairs, and he's convinced that John Rebus is dirty.  He sets out to investigate the maverick copper, and it isn't long before he learns that Rebus has a more-or-less regular drinking partner, an ex-con known as Big Ger Cafferty.  Is Rebus passing inside information to the former crime boss?  To Fox, the issue is clear: \\"The force had spent generations tolerating and turning a blind eye to cops like Rebus... Rebus was the last.  He had to be convinced that his time was past.\\"  But is Fox after Rebus because he thinks Rebus is corrupt, or because Rebus reminds him all too much of his own failings?  And is Cafferty making sure he's seen palling around with the ex-cop in order to get revenge for Rebus's putting him away?  Before long Siobhan Clarke is drawn into the fray, steering an uneasy course between supporting Rebus and trying to keep him from further antagonizing all those around him, as she and John struggle to puzzle out their personal relationship.  Meanwhile, Cafferty plays a careful cat-and-mouse game with a local crime boss who took over his criminal ops while he was inside.<br /><br />Opening a new Rebus is akin to a heavy smoker peeling the cellophane from a new pack of cigarettes after going cold turkey for a month: you shake one out, put it to your mouth, light it and inhale deeply, savouring the aroma and letting the smoke work its way back to your throat and permeate your body.  You exhale, taking your time, and once again, you're hooked.  Rankin's latest will strike his fans much the same way, only without the guilt.<br /><br />The Rebus series has long been a benchmark for contemporary noir, and much of its appeal lies in Rankin's masterful use of layered sub-plots, Rebus's nuanced relationship with Siobhan Clarke, his taut alliance with former crime boss Big Ger Cafferty, and the tension between Rebus and the force--personified in the distrust Malcolm Fox has for him.  These elements are every bit as compelling as the main story line, and raise Rankin to the very pinnacle of crime writers.  His latest is very much up to the mark. 1743117094|4.0|The Secret Keeper by Kate Morton is one of those kind of books that you anticipate as a \\"good thing\\" coming from a favorite author---a writer that knows you are just waiting for one of those giant tomes that you can muddle over for at least a week, comfortably fit into the time and place and readily identify with the angst of the main characters.  As such, this latest by Kate Morton, does not disappoint if you give it a chance and do not abandon it after the first few chapters.<br /><br />I say this because Morton's lead character placed in the timeslot of the present (2011), Laurel Nicholson, is a hard person to really get to know.  Sure, the reader is introduced to her as one of those quintessentially British actresses with the staying power of a Joan Plowright who can mold and shape herself into the likes of any Shakespearean, Dickensian or Austinian character.  The reader is told rather than feels the need to love her as a national treasure, unconditionally gushing about any comedy or tragedy into which this sort of actress devotes time and effort.<br /><br />However, as a person and a narrator, as the older sister of a clan of sisters watching their beloved mother's health fail and attempting to deal with her imminent death, Laurel seems driven by a mission; she is hell-bent on discovering her mother Dolly's big secret, relating from the first page a pivotal incident from her teenage years when her otherwise benign mother actually plunges a knife into an unwelcome visitor's neck resulting in his death.  Laurel serves as little else--a chronicler who reports her findings to the reader as she discovers them.  Morton presents Laurel as a woman in her 60s--accomplished, talented, revered and graceful.  However, she has no personal life other than that of divining her mother's past.  In this sense, Laurel acts only as the voyeuristic conduit to the era of the early 40s where her mother Dolly and her cronies battle through the Blitz in London, but as she has no other purpose, she seems wasted--minted only as the discoverer rather than having a more private and personal open-parenthesis of her own to close.  Indeed, she has puzzled over the mystery of her mother and the murdered man for over 40 years, but to truly endow her with a more faceted persona some denouement for herself besides the peace of her mother as she approaches her death is definitely necessary to make this character more engaging.<br /><br />Nonetheless, once the reader gets beyond the fact that Laurel technically just provides the glimpse into the past rather than catapulting him or her in a sub-story of self-growth, the history of the 40s Dolly, Jimmy and Vivian comes into focus as their perspectives are made known in the usual technique of alternating chapters where the reader moves backwards and forwards from one time slot to another as the intrepid Laurel uncovers more and more information from historic archives, interviews and attic rummaging.<br /><br />For the most part the structure works well--this reviewer far more enjoyed the recitation of Dolly's dreams and schemes during wartime London than the musings of Laurel over the breach of time.  However, when a third voice is added to the setup and Vivian, Dolly's friend and neighbor throws her two cents into the pot towards the end of the story, I thought Morton broke with her designated form and input an incongruous third opinion that weakened the interplay between Dolly and her Jimmy and the future observations of Laurel as Sherlockian sleuth.  Of course, Morton has her reason for including this insider's look into Vivian's mindset.  Nonetheless, technically, the duet of perspectives seems jarred by the inclusion of a third and perhaps, it should have been added from the start so that the reader could see a chronological progression instead of a seemingly red herring of counterpoint that Vivian's interjection brings.  Despite its clumsiness, Morton resolves this shift quickly as she brings her Blitz portion of the story to a close, continuing on in the future as Laurel finally uncovers the whole of the mystery with a rapidity like a snowball tumbling down hill gathering up the vital facts like snow picked up from its path.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Kate Morton writes an engrossing story for those who enjoy her genre of romantic/family historic/fiction of Rachel Hore ([[ASIN:0805094490 A Place of Secrets: A Novel]]), Katherine Webb, ([[ASIN:0062077309 The Legacy: A Novel]], [[ASIN:0062077880 The Unseen: A Novel]] and Kimberley Freeman ([[ASIN:1451623496 Wildflower Hill]]).  Unlike her best work, \\"[[ASIN:1416550550 The Forgotten Garden: A Novel]],\\" \\"The Secret Keeper\\" asks for more time to be likeable.  Her lead character Laurel Nicholson has little to offer readers other than as their conduit into the past where the real story and its denouement take place.  Her other lead, Dolly, enigmatically seems agreeable for one moment and then like a chimera morphs into a two-dimensional cartoon that has one scratching his/her head in wonder as to the character's uncanny ability to deceive one's perception.  Morton's awkward structuring of this novel lends to a bit of further confusion that does lead to a pleasantly satisfying ending that underscores an \\"ah-ha\\" moment that happily makes itself known at the novel's climax and not excruciatingly beforehand.  Recommended in its audio version performed by the fabulous Caroline Lee who also read Kimberley Freeman's [[ASIN:1451623496 Wildflower Hill]].<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"rtion of the story to a close, continuing on in the future as Laurel finally uncovers the whole of the mystery with a rapidity like a snowball tumbling down hill gathering up the vital facts like snow picked up from its path.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Kate Morton writes an engrossing story for those who enjoy her genre of romantic/family historic/fiction of Rachel Hore ([[ASIN:0805094490 A Place of Secrets: A Novel]]), Katherine Webb, ([[ASIN:0062077309 The Legacy: A Novel]], [[ASIN:0062077880 The Unseen: A Novel]] and Kimberley Freeman ([[ASIN:1451623496 Wildflower Hill]]).  Unlike her best work, \\"[[ASIN:1416550550 The Forgotten Garden: A Novel]],\\" \\"The Secret Keeper\\" asks for more time to be likeable.  Her lead character Laurel Nicholson has little to offer readers other than as their conduit into the past where the real story and its denouement take place.  Her other lead, Dolly, enigmatically seems agreeable for one moment and then like a chimera morphs into a two-dimensional cartoon that has one scratching his/her head in wonder as to the character's uncanny ability to deceive one's perception. B007V65R54|4.0|I had high hopes for this book. A book on forecasting by someone who has actually been successful at it-what could be better than that.<br /><br />===The Good Stuff===<br /><br />* Silver is a fairly honest writer. He is able to describe his previous forecasts, the methodology he used, and is frank with himself and the reader about what failed and what worked.<br /><br />* Unlike some other books on this subject, Silver comes across as actually understanding the theory behind probability, and knows how to apply it to everyday problems.<br /><br />* The material is chosen from a variety of topics, with different constraints and limitations. Chess, which is relatively deterministic but has a large number of permutations is very different that weather, which follows a few simple laws but requires intensive calculation to forecast. Silver explains how both of these create different challenges, and techniques for overcoming those problems.<br /><br />* He avoids the usual \\"bag of red and black ping-pong ball\\" nonsense that usually clutters up books on this subject, and his discussion of Bayesian probability analysis is first-rate.<br /><br />===The Not-So-Good Stuff===<br /><br />* I found the book a little tough to read. Silver writes well enough, but the material is presented in a much drier and more formal manner than other books such as Freakonomics. This is my major issue with the book. It is not a \\"mass market, fun to read\\" book, but neither is it a rigorous treatment of a mathematical subject. Rather it sort of languishes in a no-mans-land between the two. And for those of us who at least think we understand statistics, some of the passages in the book are frustrating as you try to decode what Silver is really talking about.<br /><br />* Silver has a bad habit of interjecting his own opinions and thoughts into the discussion. For example, his discussion of economic forecasting gets mired down in his own opinions on government spending levels and priorities. It takes away from the objectivity, and I know Silver knows better.<br /><br />===Conclusion===<br /><br />Even though it took me over a week to read this book, (a very long time for me), I enjoyed most of it. I did learn a few things about how forecasts work, and how to spot the difficulties in forecasting any given event. However it is more of a high-level look, and you will not learn much about how to actually forecast anything from this book.<br /><br />I would have preferred the book to either be more entertaining (Moneyball), or more \\"textbooky\\" rather than trying to be a little of both. In some ways, I think I would have enjoyed it more if I knew less about the math of statistics. Still, if you are at all interested in how complicated processes like weather, chess and national economies are forecast, it is well worth a read.es away from the objectivity, and I know Silver knows better.<br /><br />===Conclusion===<br /><br />Even though it took me over a week to read this book, (a very long time for me), I enjoyed most of it. I did learn a few things about how forecasts work, and how to spot the difficulties in forecasting any given event. However it is more of a high-level look, and you will not learn much about how to actually forecast anything from this book.<br /><br />I would have preferred the book to either be more entertaining (Moneyball), or more \\"textbooky\\" rather than trying to be a little of both. In some ways, I think I would have enjoyed it more if I knew less about the math of statistics. Still, if you are at all interested in how complicated processes like weather, chess and national economies are forecast, it is well worth a read.

From the get go, however, I immediately started thinking that Michael let his nephew's kid use his name to sell a book. This one literally reads at a 5th grade level, and it does so with no excitement whatsoever.<br /><br />Technology: Shrinking things to 1/10 their size yet retaining all their physical properties.<br /><br />Plot: A group of students of different disciplines who hang out and pursue their various degrees -- apparently in the same lab -- are given the proposition of doing research for a new biotech company. These main characters are shoved down our throats before the story starts with a list of their diverse areas of expertise, one of which is being a martial arts expert (great, that really adds depth to the character). Obviously they are shrunk and must survive to grow back. It's Timeline meets Jurassic Park meets Honey I Shrunk the Kids, all of which do way better than this.<br /><br />Problems:<br /><br />- The author does nothing with what could have been an incredible story.<br />- He throws out cliche suspense gimmicks left and right.<br />- The characters have no personality. Peter is empathetic and a leader (yeah, we get it, you don't have to keep telling us by how he likes to put his hand on everyone's shoulders)<br />- The author forgets he's the narrator, so characters often know what other characters are doing or thinking. For example, Drake knew specifically about the ants even though he never mentioned them earlier. Before that, it was just \\"dangers\\".<br />- He frequently jumps in as narrator and feeds the reader information out of the blue.<br />- Half of the book is repeating the obvious or something that had already been said (they were tired, they decided to sleep, they slept). He does this all the time.<br />- Many sections/encounters have a deux machina.<br />- The insect fights have no imagination.<br />- The excessive gore adds nothing and in fact detracts from the plot.<br />- The portrayal of the bad guy is so caricature, it's less believable than the shrinking technology.<br />- The excessive use of anthropomorphism comes across as a veiled ecological statement, not a story feature<br /><br />I lasted, I really did until almost the end, whence I put the book down, since it was obvious any ending couldn't make up for the rest. 0743297334|3.0|You'd think that a story centered on a Eunuch who loves a sexually liberal woman would be enjoyable, but it's not. Hemmingway's first novel, The Sun Also Rises, is anticlimactic and uninteresting, however his writing style is a treat, which leads me to the conclusion that although Hemmingway had a talent for writing effective sentences, at this point in his career he hadn't quite become a good storyteller.<br /><br />The story brought the reader along on a ride of people with money, no morality to drive them, too much time on their hands and a bordom of what to do with themselves. So the story is essentially made up of the characters going to clubs, and restaurants, arguing/joking in a drunken stupor and then eventually deciding to go do the same thing during the bullfights in Spain. That may sound interesting but in Spain it's the same story, they do nothing new, and there is no climax. The only thing close to a climax is that Brett cheats on Michael with another man, but the way he describes their interaction is dull and he doesn't present it in a way that hooks you. It was way too obvious and you knew it was coming.<br /><br />As for the writing, there is a lot to learn from. In some ways it shows how impactful terse sentences can be. There were times when I was caught off guard by the imagery that came to mind through his simple statements, but that can only be appreciated for so long. The long descriptions of Spanish countryside for instance made an already slow story come to a complete halt.<br /><br />So to sum it up, the story is slow and nothing that anyone in LA or modern times hasn't seen a million times over. The one thing I find interested is that I didn't realize that society then was so similar to society today: so hopeless, hedonistic and foolish in nature. Let me put it this way, if you aren't looking to be entertained, but would like literary insight on creating terse, yet impactful sentences, then this is the book for you. The book is a piece of history in Hemmingway's career when he hadn't quite found something interesting to say, but it's interesting none-the-less because of the way he says it.

159420411X|5.0|This book explains the unerring accuracy for Nate SIlver's election predictions using Bayesian statistics. The BEST part of the book for me was that I finally understand Bayes' analysis.  I  used quite a few sophisticated statistical tools in my work (retired as reliability physics expert for semiconductor devices, aka chips), but I was never able to grasp Bayes Theorem until now.  Wikipedia's \\"tutorial\\" was far too complicated even for a PhD, but Nate provided a simple version that a layman can understand ... and he did it using a hilarious example (look for \\"cheating\\").  In fact, I am so impressed with Bayes' analysis that I am thinking about writing a corollary to my two best technical papers grafting a Bayesian view.<br /> Returning to the election prediction issue, consider that each poll of 1000 people has a sampling error of +-5%, easily derived from Poisson statistics. However, when one pools the results from say 25 polls (and removes bias), the sample size is increased by 25-fold, which reduces the sampling error by 5-fold, down to +-1%.  Thus, one can make confident predictions over differences FAR smaller than the usual sampling error.  When one combines Bayesian pooling with a state-by-state analysis, one can make astonishingly accurate predictions ... Nate predicted ALL 50 states correctly, so his electoral count was exactly on reality as well when fractional electoral counts are eliminated.<br /> Buy the book as it is educational and fun to read.

0871404133|4.0|In The Social Conquest of Earth, Wilson expounds upon the theories that were set forth in his classic work Sociobiology. His main thesis is that group selection, not kin selection, drove evolution and helped us to develop societies. He compares the way human society developed to the way ant \\"society\\" developed (ants are his specialty). He suggests reasons why religion and xenophobia would have originally developed as protective characteristics of groups. This book covers a large swath of material...from ants to human prehistory, to history, to today. I think he did a pretty good job organizing the book considering what a wide topic he was covering. His theories were clear and for the most part convincing. I think Wilson is an atheist, but he did a pretty good job of stating his opinions in an agnostic sort of way to avoid insulting the faithful. I think the book was well-written, interesting, and approachable by a non-scientific audience. I had no issues with Hogan's narration--he read the book well, but it wasn't anything worth raving about.

B0067LPVCI|5.0|Before the making of this, their second album (RCA Victor LPM/LSP 3766), original Jefferson Airplane drummer Alexander \\"Skip\\" Spence had departed sometime in 1966, replaced by Spencer Dryden, and former vocalist with San Francisco's The Great Society, Grace Slick, had joined that same fall as their new lead vocalist, thereby giving this quintessential psychedelic rock band a decidedly different sound.<br /><br />Released in February 1967, and quickly finding favour in California's counter-culture movement, it soon reached # 3 on Billboard's album Top 200 and by July had gained gold status. It also produced two Billboard Pop Hot 100 singles - their first on those charts - and one near-miss. The results of their initial single, My Best Friend, were not exactly encouraging as the best it could do early in 1967 was a # 103 on the Hot 100 Bubble Under charts b/w How Do You Feel? on RCA Victor 9063.<br /><br />But then came one Grace had cut with The Great Society in 1966 on North Beach 1001 called Someone To Love, penned by her brother-in-law Darby Slick. Changing the title to Somebody To Love, it became their best hit ever in their guise as Jefferson Airplane as it peaked at # 3 Hot 100 in April/May 1967 on RCA Victor 9140 b/w She Has Funny Cars. That was followed in July by the # 8 Hot 100 White Rabbit on RCA Victor 9248 b/w Plastic Fantastic Lover. These would be the only two times they'd crack the Top 10 as Jefferson Airplane, although they'd do it six more times as Jefferson Starship/Starship, including two # 1s in the 1980s.<br /><br />Lingering controversy over whether or not Grateful Dead guitarist Jerry Garcia had a hand in the album continues unabated, but on the reverse cover RCA prints \\"Jerry Garcia, musical and spiritual adviser\\" among the credits - something always denied by album producer Rick Jarrard. In any event, Rolling Stone magazine lists Surrealistic Pillow at # 146 on their \\"500 Greatest Albums of All Time.\\"<br /><br />At the time the band consisted of: lead guitarist/vocalist Jorma Kaukonen; vocalist/guitarist Marty Balin; lead vocalist/pianist Grace Slick (also organ and recorder); rhythm guitarist/vocalist Paul Kantner; Jack Casady on bass, fuzz bass and rhythm guitar; and drummer/percussionist Spender Dryden.<br /><br />The tracks, with writers shown in brackets, are:<br />Side 1<br />1. B006OAB3ME|4.0|I have heard Rod Stewart attempt to sing songs from the decades before rock took over the pop charts. I say attempt because it fell flat in my estimation. Paul McCartney on the other hand is actually pretty good at this. He's no Frank Sinatra, but Frank would never be able to sing Live and Let Die if he wanted.<br /><br />Paul sings in a softer, often higher voice than I would have expected. That takes a little getting used to. On some songs I might have suggested a different key for Paul. As others have said, the backup and arrangements are tops. I would have enjoyed more instrumental soloing from this band, given the caliber of the talent, but the producers no doubt insisted the focus should be on the singing.<br /><br />Not every track is a success. Bye Bye Blackbird sung at a dirge-like tempo may be unique, but Ringo Starr's version (on his Sentimental Journey album) is actually more effective. Paul wrote two originals for this album, and while My Valentine is nicely crafted, the lyrics to Only Our Hearts are pretty bad. (That is unfortunate, as the music was wonderful.)<br /><br />The standout tracks for me, the ones I thought Paul interpreted most effectively, are Home, More I Cannot Wish You, My Valentine, Always, and Inch Worm.<br /><br />Another two, which I enjoy immensely, were left off the standard issue of the album, and are available only on the Deluxe Edition sold exclusively in Target stores (though it is available on amazon at an inflated price). Baby's Request is one of those old-timey sounding numbers that Paul used to write from time to time (Honey Pie with the Beatles, You Gave Me The Answer with Wings). Originally Baby's Request appeared on Paul's generally rocking Back To The Egg album. Here it gets the classy treatment it deserves. It's not a great song, but very fun, and performed perfectly here. The last exclusive track on the Deluxe album is My One And Only Love. When I first heard it, I could not believe this fantastic performance/arrangement was left off the standard issue of the album. One And Only Love may be McCartney's best vocal of all the sessions for this album. If you need a reason to go to the store and buy the Deluxe edition, for me, this is it. There are two Deluxe editions available just to make things confusing, so make sure the one you buy has Baby's Request and My One And Only Love as the final two tracks (16 tracks total).<br /><br />In spite of a few songs that really don't appeal to me, there are enough winners to justify four stars. If you love McCartney and the kinds of standards that Sinatra used to sing, there's no reason not to add this to your collection. Without the Deluxe tracks I mentioned, I would not be quite as enthusiastic, though the standard 14-track release is still a decent collection.ment was left off the standard issue of the album. 0452277752|5.0|As Bachman, King seems to write much about versions of dystopia, whether it be visions of the future or the lead character's own personal world gone wrong and how they deal with it. The Bachman books, much the same as many of King's books, have as their protagonist; outsiders, loners and underdogs, which I inadvertently find myself rooting for, no matter what character flaws they may have.<br /><br />I picked up a compendium of four of the Bachman stories for 1 at a charity book sale. The four stories being: `Rage', `The Long Walk', `Roadwork' and the reason I picked the book up in the first place... `The Running Man'.<br /><br />'Rage'<br /><br />The first of the Bachman books sees the main character, Charlie Decker, a senior at high school, called from his class by the headteacher to discuss an incident where he had assaulted and seriously injured one of his teachers. During this meeting, Charlie snaps, is expelled from school and he then leaves the headmaster's office and retrieves a pistol from his locker. Things go from bad to worse for all involved...<br /><br />Perhaps the greatest point of note about this particular tale is that King is no longer comfortable with it being in print after its association with incidents supposedly inspired by the contents of the story... King himself has publically spoken about the novel and its influence: \\"The Carneal incident [another US school shooting where `Rage' was allegedly an influence] was enough for me. I asked my publisher to take the damned thing out of print. They concurred.\\"<br /><br />For me, Rage is not the best of the Bachman books but is an interesting little story that could have easily been written by another author. Despite its apparent influences on maladjusted individuals, it is my opinion that it is still quite a powerful short story and comments succinctly on adolescent frustration, social class division and bullying among other issues.<br /><br />'The Long Walk'<br /><br />Set in a dystopian, militaristic and totalitarian America of the not too distant future, The Long Walk refers to a wildly popular \\"sport\\" which sees 100 teenage boys enter and participate in the titular annual contest. Sounds very much like The Hunger Games, right?! The rules are fairly simple: walkers must maintain an average walking speed of 4mph, not stop, not sleep, do anything to deviate from the route set or they will receive a warning. Receiving three warnings will result in being \\"ticketed\\". Given that there are soldiers in vehicles riding alongside the walkers, it soon becomes abundantly clear to the reader what the meaning of that third warning is. The winner of the contest and recipient of \\"the prize\\" is the last boy left alive.<br /><br />The popularity of this contest is akin to the gameshows seen in `The Running Man' (see review in Part II or refer to the Arnie movie!). Such shows seem to be an effort to stupify the masses, give them hope and take their minds off the misery of their own lives, much the same way Roman emperors used the Colosseum or ITV uses the X-Factor. Perhaps it's just the cynic in me but this particular story seems to be particularly apt when in this time of austerity and cutbacks, the Government is spending millions gearing up for the Olympic and Commonwealth Games by building new facilities and stadia across the country and urging the nation to get behind this initiative.<br /><br />Again, I don't want to give too much of the story away but this is a close second to my favourite out of these books and it would appear that Frank Darabont (long time King fan and Director of the screen adaptations of The Shawshank Redemption, The Green Mile and The Mist) is a fan also. Darabont has secured the screen rights to The Long Walk.<br /><br />This short story held my attention considerably and images conjured up in my head from the reading of it and the powerful ending remain in my mind long after the last sentence was read.<br /><br />`Roadwork'<br /><br />I feel Ihave to apologise in advance since my opinion on `Roadwork' is not a favourable one. I found this particular tale to be distinctly average and, like `Rage', King admits it was written at a time of high emotion in his own life and to my mind, is not his finest work or even indeed of the Bachman books.<br /><br />`Roadwork' sees a Mr Average American (Barton Dawes) in the suburban midwest, subjected to some of the more harrowing, yet still very real, experiences that ordinary people have to deal with in the course of their lives. Dawes loses everything and the focus of his anger at this loss becomes the new motorway being constructed and alluded to in the title, culminating in a destructive finale.<br /><br />Taken directly from the introduction to the Bachman Books, King said:<br />\\"I think it was an effort to make some sense of my mother's painful death the year before - a lingering cancer had taken her off inch by painful inch. Following this death I was left both grieving and shaken by the apparent senselessness of it all... Roadwork tries so hard to be good and find some answers to the conundrum of human pain.\\"<br /><br />`Roadwork' is like the diet version of the Michael Douglas movie `Falling Down'. It had so much potential but I feel that King's own frame of mind at the time may have had a major part to play in the feel of the story. Readers may find some sort of allegorical interpretation in what King has set out here but for me, it simply didn't hit the mark.<br /><br />'The Running Man'<br /><br />As stated earlier, this is the reason I shelled out one whole Queen's Head for this book. Raised on a diet of Arnie, Van Damme and Seagal movies, I had to read this book!<br /><br />The book however, is distinctly different, darker and far superior to the movie loosely based on it...<br /><br />Set in America in the year 2025, `The Running Man' sees the US economy in ruins, its citizens living in abject poverty and entertained by the violent and often lethal gameshows provided by The Games Network; `The Running Man' is the most popular of these said shows. The format of the game is distinctly different than that shown in the Arnie flick; contestants choose to enter the game, usually through desperation for cash; Contestants can travel anywhere they choose (globally) but there are \\"Hunters\\" tracking them down and will kill the contestants once caught. Contestants earn $100 for every hour they evade capture but must also submit videos on a regular basis to prove they are alive and remain in the game. Additionally, contestants will receive $100 for every law enforcement officer or Hunter that they kill. Manage to survive 30 days, the contestant receives $1billion.<br /><br />So, already some differences from the movie, notably no \\"game areas\\". Also, the Hunters aren't brightly costumed freakshows with unique weaponry and gadgets; to my mind, it is more terrifying that the Hunters wear no mark or clue as to reveal their identity and carry concealed weapons, allowing them to travel anonymously while they hunt for contestants.<br /><br />Ben Richards, the focus of the novel, is one such contestant. A man driven to enter in order to pay for medicine for his dying daughter and in an effort to stop his wife \\"turning tricks\\" to get what medicine she could. King has stated that the Richards of the book is \\"...as far away from the Arnold Schwarzenegger character in the movie as you can get.\\" and described him as \\"scrawny\\" and \\"pre-tubercular\\"!<br /><br />There is no question that `The Running Man' is an action driven story. However, it is much grittier than the movie of the same name and far more cerebral. The reader can't help but empathise with Richards' predicament; his desperation is palpable; his paranoia and suspicion while \\"playing the game\\" is very real and his determination to finish what he started has you rooting for him all the way to the book's end which could not be more different from the movie; and makes for a chillingread given that it mirrors events that take place in reality approximately twenty years later...<br /><br />King allegedly wrote this story in only 3 days. `The Running Man' reads as a fast-paced, action story set in a future gone wrong but I would never have guessed that King brought this all together in such a short space of time. As previously stated, this story is the reason I bought the book and is the tale that I chose to read first and of the four, it is my firm favourite.<br /><br />The Bachman Books, in my opinion, are rightly categorised as horror. All four stories have at their core, bleak views of life, whether it be the dystopian futures of `The Long Walk' and `The Running Man'; the all too real nightmare of `Rage'; or the steady slip into oblivion of `Roadwork'.shows provided by The Games Network; `The Running Man' is the most popular of these said shows. The format of the game is distinctly different than that shown in the Arnie flick; contestants choose to enter the game, usually through desperation for cash; Contestants can travel anywhere they choose (globally) but there are \\"Hunters\\" tracking them down and will kill the contestants once caught. Contestants earn $100 for every hour they evade capture but must also submit videos on a regular basis to prove they are alive and remain in the game. As previously stated, this story is the reason I bought the book and is the tale that I chose to read first and of the four, it is my firm favourite.<br /><br />The Bachman Books, in my opinion, are rightly categorised as horror. All four stories have at their core, bleak views of life, whether it be the dystopian futures of `The Long Walk' and `The Running Man'; the all too real nightmare of `Rage'; or the steady slip into oblivion of `Roadwork'.

These families are first introduced in \\"Fall of the Giants\\"  and this novel continues the tale of their evolutions.  The novel, if it was a film, would probably be described as a docudrama, i.e., it uses literary and narrative techniques to flesh out the bare facts of events in history to tell the story.  Indeed the novel covers a period from 1933 starting with the ascendency of Adolf Hitler to chancellor of Germany and the rise of Nazism to 1949 when the Russians demonstrate (explode) their first atomic bomb.  Many of the major twentieth century figures and events are chronicled in this book.  Political actors include: Roosevelt, Stalin, Churchill, Hitler. Scientific actors include : Leslie Groves, J. Robert Oppenheimer, Enrico Fermi; Some of the events woven into the plot are: Pearl Harbor, Normandy, Manhattan Project. However, the fundamental essence of the book revolves around love, both fulfilled and unfulfilled;  disappointment and triumph; compassion and cruelty; loyalty and treason; and hate and humanity. B0081BU42O|4.0|It's been too long since I read a new Iain M. Banks Culture story.  This one has many of the elements that make the Culture interesting.  There are deep-thinking and meddlesome Ship Minds with deliciously-improbable names (e.g., Mistake Not...*) who charge about the universe and dispatch their avatars to interact with mere humans.  There are characters with odd body modifications, including a musician with an extra pair of arms and one male character with 52 extra... appendages.  And there is the expected Culture tech such as knife missiles, full mind state backup, \\"displacement,\\" and a variety of Very Large artificial habitats.<br /><br />This story showcases the \\"Subliming\\" that happens when an entire civilization decides to move on to a higher plane of existence.  A Sublimed civilization disappears from the physical universe, with individuals likely merging to form a single, powerful entity.  Nobody is quite sure how this happens.  It is similar to the process of a civilization moving into the \\"Transcend\\" in Vernor Vinge's [[ASIN:B0047CPB2Y Zones of Thought]] series.  Banks has referred to Sublimation in other Culture books, but much has been left unsaid.  This book scratches our itch for a closer look.<br /><br />The Gzilt civilization is preparing to Sublime.  Individual Gzilt are getting their affairs in order and saying their good-byes to some who will stay behind as the waning \\"Gzilt Remnant.\\"  Various scavenger civilizations probe the edges of Gzilt space, hoping to make a fast grab for abandoned technology and territory.  Amid these preparations, a long-kept secret may be revealed.  It seems that the Gzilt Book of Truth was a hoax perpetrated by the Zidhren civilization.  Revelation of this secret may threaten the unity needed by the Gzilt to Sublime.<br /><br />Drawn into the ensuing conflict, musician and reserve Lieutenant Commander Vyr Cossont finds allies among both the Culture and the Gzilt.  She searches for 9,000-year-old Ngaroe QiRia, who was present at the founding of the Culture and may know why the Gzilt decided not to join it.  As Cossont searches with increasing desperation, the book's chapters count down to the time chosen for Subliming.<br /><br />This is a gotta-read for those who follow the Culture.  And it's a good, accessible story for any science fiction fan reading Banks for the first time. 0316228532|1.0|I really wanted this book to be so much more than it was. It's hard to top yourself when you start out with something like the Harry Potter series, and it's understandable that Rowling would want to distance herself from that genre as an author, but its almost as if she wasn't even trying for a good story here. It had none of her charm, her humor, her voice. Rowling is trying to make a political statement here and maybe she accomplished what she intended to, but it was far from an enjoyable read.<br /><br />The book started out slow and extremely detailed. Right away all of the characters (there are many) were unlikable. I kept waiting for them to redeem themselves as human beings, but it never happened. I kept waiting for the mood to lighten up, for someone to crack a joke, but no. Even the sympathetic characters, and it took me a while into the book before I even figured out which a-holes I was supposed to be rooting for, acted like jerks to the people around them. Perhaps JK Rowling was attempting to portray the misery of the human condition in general. If so, good job, but it wasn't pleasant to read. I would never want to go to Pagford or have anything to do with any of the characters, and the thought of people like that actually existing is horribly depressing. The whole premise is mundane and boring sounding, but I was so hoping the in the hands of the right author it could be humorous. No. It was all the unpleasantness of actual small town politics in book form.<br /><br />The detailed scenes, many of which I considered unnecessary to the plot continued all the way to the end. It feels as though she didn't bother with editing. JK Rowling is a good writer, and what she has a way of bringing a scene to life in a unique way. Her writing style was the only reason I finished this book at all. Any other writer and I would have thrown it against a wall long before the end. It did feel as if she were throwing big words around without purpose just to show off. I love a good vocabulary but much of it felt out of context.<br /><br />The ending was emotional, but I had no sense that anything had been accomplished, that any of the characters had learned anything, I just felt depressed. Life is shitty and then it gets worse. Everyone hates everyone else, and attempted to make life miserable for everyone around them all the time. No one connected with anyone else on a significant emotional level. I wouldn't reccomend this book for anyone. It wasn't fun. It was far too long and it went nowhere. The only redeeming thing about The Casual Vacancy is its author. 0812984412|4.0|Cloud Atlas is one part Rubik's Cube, one part Zen Koan.  It's a novel, but it is also a collection of madly different stories that are loosely tied together through time and space.  (I use the term `loosely' loosely).  More than once, while reading Cloud Atlas, I wondered how I could get a glass of whatever David Mitchell was drinking, in hopes that I might stumble upon the secret to possessing such an unfettered imagination.  I, too, wanted to soar with the eagles of possibility and sink to the darkest recesses of the sea of despair without a backward glance at who was following.    I read the first two stories in this fragmented opus, \\"The Pacific Journal of Adam Ewing\\" and \\"Letters from Zedelghem\\", with a dictionary at my side, racking up new vocabulary words with every other sentence.  I could not fathom where some of those sentences came from, or where they were going, for that matter.  I didn't let it bother me.  I bit my nails throughout the third story, \\"Half-Lives: The First Louisa Rey Mystery\\".  The fourth story, \\"The Ghastly Ordeal of Timothy Cavendish\\", made me laugh out loud.  The fifth, \\"An Orison of Sonmi-451\\", I read with open-mouthed wonder.  It was all I could do to just keep up.  I read the sixth story with my eyes crossed, allowing me to hear the words in my head as much as read them with my eyes. That story, \\"Sloosha's Crossin' an' Ev'rythin' After\\", breaks the main rule about writing in dialect.  The one that states: Thou Shall Not Write in Dialect.  Unless you are David Mitchell, apparently.    And all of that only gets you to the halfway mark.  You'll have to read it yourself to find out where it goes from there.  Hopefully you are getting the idea that this book is all over the map linguistically, crosses genres without a thought, and challenges the reader in most every way imaginable.    But wait!  In addition to all this fancy trans-genre, trans-lingual writing going on, there's a theme.  An earth shaking, eye opening, heart breaking theme.  In fact, there are several, and all of them are deep, meaningful, and fully integral to the story(s).  Of course Mitchell is getting at the connectedness of everything:  he is oh-so-clever about tying these stories together, threading the characters through time, and making their singular struggles eternal.  But this book is also about the evolution of our species, the result and byproducts of the white man's conquest of the world, and the karmic wheel of fate.  That, and about ten other deeply profound things that just make you jealous of David Mitchell's creative mind.    You can see my full review, and other reviews and book suggestions, on my blog:  [...]

B008EKON56|3.0|I bought this adapter to try something: I wanted to set up my iPod nano on my desk, the output going through a small amplifier to some desktop speakers, without routing through my PC. I also wanted to have the iPod be charged constantly so I didn't have to unclip it everyday to recharge it. There are two signal outputs available on the iPod:<br /><br />1-the 'internal' output, which comes through the base adapter and can be intercepted either through a PC or by using a commonly-available Y adapter (I was using one of those). This is a signal output only, fixed volume, is not controlled by the volume/menu dial on the iPod, and bypasses all the iPod internal equalizer (EQ) settings including such things as 'enhanced bass' or volume gain control; it is raw signal only. Apple intended your PC audio player (presumably under iTunes) to do that.<br />2-the 1/8\\" miniature stereo plug that appears on the bottom of the iPod alongside the base adapter slot. This is intended for the earbuds/earphones and *does* use the EQ, volume and gain settings.<br /><br />I wanted the EQ settings and the volume gain controls to work, and to use the iPod volume dial rather than the volume dial on the amp (I hoped it wouldn't be necessary given the EQ gain settings anyway).<br /><br />I thought that I'd use this adapter to see if there was any difference in audio output from the Y adapter I was using (they are of notoriously poor construction). There is none, this adapter operates identically to the Y adapter, with the exception that you need a power source that ends with a mini-USB terminal (not the thinner micro as is on most all cellphones these days) rather than the Apple dock cord with their proprietary adapter, which of course is built into the top of this adapter.<br /><br />BTW, in case there's any confusion, the manufacturer shows two views of the one adapter in the same photo so you can see both the power input (a mini USB) and the audio output (mini-stereo) that are located on opposite sides (there's nothing on the bottom of the adapter), you don't get two adapters. The placement of the mini-USB power input on the adapter blocks the headphone audio output on the iPod so that it cannot be used, I don't think that was the intent but it is placed that way just the same, thus you can only use the 'internal' line-audio. The audio output on this adapter is the internal, fixed, signal-only that bypasses all the EQ, volume and gain controls, it is useful as a feed for your car or amplifier with EQ, volume and gain controls, not headphones.<br /><br />The adapter is fine for what it does, and seems reasonably well built, but except for compactness and looks, offers no particular advantage over the much less costly Y adapters.

0785163174|1.0|Beware of some spoilerish information:<br /><br />I really thought that something like FLASHPOINT or SECRET WARS II or BLOODLINES or ATLANTIS ATTACKS would be the bottom of the barrel as far as major company superhero \\"Event\\" comics could go. I thought that things would get better. AVENGERS VS. X-MEN, on paper, had everything going for it: some of the best writers at Marvel (Brian Bendis, Ed Brubaker, Jonathan Hickman, Jason Aaron, and Matt Fraction) along with some great artists (John Romita Jr., Olivier Coipel, and Andy Kubert) coming together to pit the two big teams of the Marvel Universe against each other as the threat of The Phoenix Force looms over them all. Sounds like this should be a huge winner, right?<br /><br />Right?<br /><br />Well, as it turns out, the brain trust over at Marvel (or their \\"Architects\\") went into full-on Battle Mode PVP-style writing with little-to-no regard for any real characterization. It's meant to be a clash for survival, but it bobs and weaves through these characters and tries to present it as a clash of ideologies. And when all is said and done, it's really just an excuse for heroes to clash, and the writers have to work backwards from that idea and hope that it all actually makes for a compelling story.<br /><br />SPOILER ALERT: It doesn't. In any way. At all.<br /><br />Cyclops, leader of the X-Men, has determined that Hope, the only mutant born after the whole HOUSE OF M debacle where The Scarlet Witch decided in her nervous breakdown that there should be \\"No More Mutants\\", will be the vessel in which The Phoenix Force will reside and it will literally give hope for the eviscerated mutant population. Captain America and Iron Man, over on The Avengers side, see The Phoenix Force only as a force of destruction since several years before, Jean Grey played host to The Phoenix Force, and as Dark Phoenix, she destroyed a planet with billions of lives snuffed out.<br /><br />Now it seems that the pragmatic thing to do would be to have a summit or conversation of sorts between these two insanely powerful groups rather than risking another CIVIL WAR-type confrontation of Hero Against Hero. But these are superhero comics. And apparently, in superhero comics, you can't actually talk about things; you have to fight about it. Captain America and most of the Avengers (including the New Avengers and Secret Avengers) come to Utopia, basically to take Hope into custody and try and figure out what the next course of action is to stop The Phoenix Force. At least, that's how they make it try and sound at first. When Cyclops tells them to leave since The Phoenix Force has always been a mutant issue, Cap comes off like a fascist, jack-booted thug and tells Cyclops that if they don't comply, they'll take Hope by force.<br /><br />Well, the optic blast goes flying and that's it. The Avengers and The X-Men are at war. But things start turning really bad, not only in the actual story, but in the storytelling, and that's what's important. Perhaps one of the worst things they could have done is have involved all these writers. Writing by committee is historically not a good thing. Character voices and intentions change; additional plots get tacked on that service the writer's voice at the expense of plot and character development. After all, this thing has to go on for 13 issues (A #0 issue + 12 regular issues), and additionally, there HAVE to be tie-in series, and that means a series which is the ultimate in cash-grabbing and represents the worst that comics have to offer and that's the six-issue AVX: VS series, which does almost nothing to actually advance the story or give any real insight into character, but instead gives some fans the ability to \\"enjoy\\" watching Mutant A and Avenger B beating the living hell out of each other. Those issues are collected here as well. You are also treated to AVENGERS VS X-MEN INFINITE, which is the formerly digital only tie-ins to the story, which I have not read many of, but I do recall some of it having to do a little with the new Nova (man, I'm bummed that Richard Rider is gone).<br /><br />As things get increasingly insane, and as it hops back and forth between Utopia, K'un L'un, Wakanda and the Southwestern US, allegiances are broken, friendships and possibly marriages are broken beyond repair, and a \\"shocking\\" death signals a supposedly radical shift in Marvel's status quo.<br /><br />This is a series that literally showcases the absolute worst that not only all of the writers', but most of the artists' work has to offer. Romita's usually clean artwork seems hurried and scratchy. Kubert's work is good, but is nothing really special, and just downright silly in a few places. The only exception here is Coipel, who does do some really nice work.<br /><br />There is a random issue in this series (I believe it's issue 7 or 8) that is pretty good, but other than that, it's FUBAR. Bendis turns in some of the worst work here, but in peripheral issues of NEW AVENGERS, he does some incredible issues. Aaron is the only trustworthy worker bee here, who not only gives this whole series its only good issue, but his peripheral work on WOLVERINE AND THE X-MEN still remains very well done.<br /><br />Some of you reading this may surmise that I set out to hate this. Nothing could be further from the truth. I desperately wanted to enjoy it, but I saw too many characters being marginalized or made villainous, and too much confusion in the plot, where it seemed to promise one thing, but gave something else. The one thing that I will concede to this series is that it did change or, more specifically, undo something that has been status quo and can potentially create something exciting. But I don't know if the birth of those new ideas are worth the labor pains of this series.<br /><br />Now, like DC's FLASHPOINT, this \\"event\\" is paving the way for the MARVEL NOW! initiative, which will give us a lot of new #1 issues for a lot of characters and a lot of books. Of course DC's New 52, which while having nothing to do with the events of FLASHPOINT still came on the heels of FLASHPOINT, was a pretty phenomenal success as far as getting people talking about comics again and drumming up some new readers and more sales, and that's really what's at work here. The whole sound and fury of AVENGERS VS X-MEN is about the same thing: New readers, more sales. Whether or not that will actually be successful for Marvel remains to be seen.<br /><br />But if the quality of those books are anything like this, Marvel will have to count a lot more on its film and toy division to make money.<br /><br />As an addendum, I do have to say that this whole \\"clash of ideologies\\" thing as far as hero fighting hero has worked very well in the past with CIVIL WAR. It was a battle that worked on every level (with a few strange exceptions) and created a whole new conversation for the heroes to have. Despite the fact that there were some really poor character moments, it all ended when Captain America so rightly observed that they weren't fighting for any real ideology or belief anymore; he realized that they were just fighting. That's where that \\"Event\\" ended, and that was something of a brilliant ending. Here in this \\"event\\", the heroes indeed just fight, because the argument is barely there.<br /><br />AVENGERS VS X-MEN is really probably the worst major \\"event\\" comic I've ever read. And that's saying something.ut I do recall some of it having to do a little with the new Nova (man, I'm bummed that Richard Rider is gone).<br /><br />As things get increasingly insane, and as it hops back and forth between Utopia, K'un L'un, Wakanda and the Southwestern US, allegiances are broken, friendships and possibly marriages are broken beyond repair, and a \\"shocking\\" death signals a supposedly radical shift in Marvel's status quo.<br /><br />This is a series that literally showcases the absolute worst that not only all of the writers', but most of the artists' work has to offer. Romita's usually clean artwork seems hurried and scratchy. Kubert's work is good, but is nothing really special, and just downright silly in a few places. The only exception here is Coipel, who does do some really nice work.<br /><br />There is a random issue in this series (I believe it's issue 7 or 8) that is pretty good, but other than that, it's FUBAR. The whole sound and fury of AVENGERS VS X-MEN is about the same thing: New readers, more sales. Whether or not that will actually be successful for Marvel remains to be seen.<br /><br />But if the quality of those books are anything like this, Marvel will have to count a lot more on its film and toy division to make money.<br /><br />As an addendum, I do have to say that this whole \\"clash of ideologies\\" thing as far as hero fighting hero has worked very well in the past with CIVIL WAR. It was a battle that worked on every level (with a few strange exceptions) and created a whole new conversation for the heroes to have. Despite the fact that there were some really poor character moments, it all ended when Captain America so rightly observed that they weren't fighting for any real ideology or belief anymore; he realized that they were just fighting. That's where that \\"Event\\" ended, and that was something of a brilliant ending. Here in this \\"event\\", the heroes indeed just fight, because the argument is barely there.<br /><br />AVENGERS VS X-MEN is really probably the worst major \\"event\\" comic I've ever read. And that's saying something.

1616960922|4.0|As much as I've enjoyed the Mistborn saga, The Way of Kings, and his work on the final Wheel of Time novels, Elantris is one of the few works by Brandon Sanderson that I have yet to read (along with Warbreaker).<br /><br />Fortunately, while The Emperor's Soul is set in the same world as Elantris, it is a completely separate story, and doesn't require any advance knowledge of the world Sanderson has created.<br /><br />Clocking in at under 200 pages, this lacks the intense world-building of his other work, but still manages to cram in a significant amount of detail regarding his magic system. A bit more philosophical in nature, the magic of forging, involving the use of soulstamps and essence marks, is absolutely fascinating to explore. Sanderson does a masterful job of taking something simple, yet impossible, and making it a wondrously imaginative act of creation. Here, the system of magic is revealed not through narrative exposition, but through conversations between Shai, the Forger, and Gaotona, her captor. It means a slow unveiling of what she does and how she does it, but the means of that unveiling is an integral part of the tale.<br /><br />Shai herself is an interesting character, more admirable than heroic. She is a character with whom we can sympathise, but one who does not prey on our sympathies. Less a victim than an opportunistic captive, she forges the means of her escape long before the end, but cannot bring herself to flee without satisfying her curiosity as to whether her greatest forgery will work. After all, it's one thing to forge great pieces of art, or to temporarily alter one's self . . . it's another thing entirely to forge a complete stranger's soul.<br /><br />It's a shame that this is such a short work, told almost exclusively from Shai's point of view, because I would have liked to see the character of Gaotona better explored. He is intriguing, a man transparent in his emotions, but rather reserved in his motivations, and one who work as both a foil and a friend to Shai. Given the restrictions of the story, he is never really developed beyond the stern, compassionate, grandfatherly character we so often encounter in fantasy, but he is given a chance to shine in the final chapters . . . one that makes us want to know more about him.<br /><br />Like a great play, the world here is largely relegated to a single room, and the characters are limited to a handful of key players. The drama all takes place on the stage, in plain view, relying on our connection to the characters, as well as our own curiosity, to keep us in our seats. In essence, just as we come to sympathise with Shai, we also come to share her curiosity about this impossible abomination which which she has been tasked.<br /><br />While there is some significant action towards the end, with those magically-augmented battles that Sanderson writes so well, this is largely a story of ideas. It flows along at a leisurely pace, but has more than enough wit and wonder to keep the reader engaged. All-in-all a fine read for Sanderson fans new and old, and one that has put Elantris back near the top of my to-read pile.both a foil and a friend to Shai.
